Page 19

Free Talk

Examining Old Accounts 293

— Traveler Falcon

These past few days, I've heard that the Xun Society has published a book, "Words of the Meat-Eaters," which contains the current officials' pronouncements from when they were still out of power. It encourages everyone to "observe their actions based on their words," to see how different they are before and after. The weekly publication "New Voice" from the same society often has articles with the same message.

This is like examining old accounts, opening the ledgers, and tallying up the figures to give a final calculation. It asks how there can be inconsistencies between past and present, and it is indeed a most practical and clear method that leaves no room for evasion. However, this method seems a bit too "old-fashioned" for the present day.

The ancients were afraid of having these kinds of old accounts examined. Wei Zhuang of Shu, when he was poor, wrote a passionate and relatively accessible poem called "Qin Woman Now," which everyone truly passed around. But after he became prominent, he not only refused to include it in his collection, but even tried to destroy others' copies. The reason for this was unknown at the time, but seeing that this manuscript was excavated from the caves of Dunhuang at the end of the Qing Dynasty, it's clear that his efforts were in vain. Nevertheless, his anxieties are understandable.

However, this applies to famous figures of the past. Ordinary people are different. To erase their old accounts, they must cut off their heads and be reborn. When condemned criminals are taken to the execution grounds, they shout, "In twenty years, I'll be a good man again!" To start anew, they must wait twenty years, which is quite troublesome.

However, this is the case for ordinary people of both past and present. Today's celebrities are different. To erase their old accounts and start anew, their methods are as different from those of ordinary people as mail is from telegrams. If they don't mind being slow, they can go abroad once, build a temple, have an illness, or tour the mountains for a few days. If they want to be quick, they can hold a conference, recite a sutra, give a speech, make a declaration, or even just sleep for a night and write a poem. If they want to be even quicker, they can slap themselves twice, shed a few tears, and transform into a completely different person, with no connection to their "former self." General Pure Altar transforms into a carp and swims between the legs of female demons. The author may have thought he was writing something wonderfully imaginative, but from today's perspective, it lacks even a hint of novelty.

If these transformations still seem troublesome, they can roll their eyes and ask, "Are these my accounts?" If that's still too much trouble, they can skip the eye-rolling and questioning altogether. This last method is largely what's in vogue today.

How can "old-fashioned" methods be used in today's world? Some people even advocate reading the classics, which is baffling. However, overnight, they might advocate that everyone join the army, so I haven't bought any classics, and I probably won't be enlisting tomorrow either.

On "Famine" Works (Continued) 294

— Feng Wu

This "main element," which Ye Shaojun overlooked, is strongly emphasized in Bai Wei's play "Famine." However, "Famine," like "More Than Three or Five Bushels," has many flaws. Specifically, first, the author does not have a good understanding of the structure of rural society. Therefore, a "poor" peasant family can have a beautiful urban female student, merchants rely on farmers to cover up economic exploitation by the government, officials completely adopt direct persecution of farmers, wealthy gentry do not even have the courage to directly confront farmers, and the only connection to imperialism in rural bankruptcy is the shipment of rice. Second, many of the author's descriptions lack authenticity. In terms of characters, it can be said that the author's depictions are completely intellectuals in disguise, especially the "second son," who is simply a typical wavering petty bourgeois. Third, the author's story is romanticized. A poor peasant family has an urban female student, who also has a cousin who is an official, and that cousin is both pursuing the woman and attacking her family. After her family is ruined, he comes to pursue her in love. In terms of social organization, this kind of situation would never exist. Therefore, although Bai Wei's play has good intentions and involved great effort, due to insufficient understanding and lack of life experience, it inevitably has some failures.

In comparison, Mao Dun's "Autumn Harvest" is quite successful. When reading this piece in conjunction with "Spring Silkworms" and "Late Winter," at least three characteristics can be pointed out. First, it develops a depiction of the growth of the peasants. From striving for "survival" to hunger in a year of good harvest, from awareness in hunger to "eating the wealthy households" and "robbing rice stores," and from "eating the wealthy households" and "robbing rice stores" to gradual organization and "handing in firearms," it depicts the process step by step. Second, it shows the convergence of the rural hungry masses. In the novel, he not only wrote about the complexity of the peasants' consciousness, but also wrote about how peasants with such complexity gradually converged under the common oppression of hunger. Ultimately, even the extremely stubborn Old Tongbao had to say when he was dying: "I never thought you (Little Bean Head) were right. It's really strange!" Third, the author powerfully imaged everything, rather than writing mechanically. Art, of course, is imagery-based, and since many new writers cannot do this, I specially emphasize this point. For example, on the aspect of imperialist aggression against the countryside, he does not present it philosophically or with slogans, but writes about Old Tongbao's dislike of "foreign things," which is a good example. As for the fact that urban and rural areas alike have no escape and can only unite to strive for "new life," this is, of course, also one of the important advantages. However, after reading "Midnight" and these novels, I cannot help but express my feelings about the author of "Autumn Harvest," and I cannot help but have further hopes. Namely, first, as a representative of progressive writers, the author has not yet been able to find revolutionary opportunities from the myriad of realities and portray them as revolutionary themes, exerting the positiveness of the theme to complete the task that progressive writers inevitably undertake. Second, the author's non-dialectical, supra-class, purely objectivist attitude has constantly prevented the author from understanding events more deeply, and created a crisis of moving towards mechanical materialism. These two problems are also a problem of understanding and observation methods. If the author can arduously study dialectical materialism, and only dialectical materialism can overcome pure objectivism and subjectivism, I think the author's driving force for the development of the entire literary movement will be ten or a hundred times greater than it is now.

Blood Debt 295

— Ye Rong

Ah Da walked in from outside the door, nodded at me, and sat down solemnly. My wife hurriedly put down the child, got up to pour a cup of tea, and delivered it to the table with a smiling face, saying, "Master, have some tea."

I immediately understood why he had come. I lowered my head and twisted the straw rope for making a winnowing basket, waiting for him to speak first. I didn't know how I could send him away today. Ah Da wasn't easy to talk to; he had a menacing face.

He picked up the tea from the table, took a sip, and his lips trembled a few times, as if the tea had stung him. He opened his mouth hesitantly:

"Monk, I, I've come, you, you know why. That one, one hundred yuan in rent you owe, today you promise tomorrow, tomorrow, tomorrow you promise the day after tomorrow. When, when will you, will you pay? You're making me run back and forth."

I felt speechless. Every time he came, I used up all my good words, and repeated them so many times that even I felt pressured. And as soon as I opened my mouth, he would cut me off, saying, "I've heard enough of this. You, you just tell me, do, do you have it today?" I'd heard enough of his words too.

"Why don't you, don't you answer?" He started to get angry, pounding the table. My wife was frightened, and the child was startled by the noise and started to cry.

"Master!" My wife called out to him timidly, but couldn't say anything more, as if begging him to spare me, or perhaps asking him not to bang on the table and scare the child.

"You, you don't answer, won't, won't do. Boss, says, if you don't have it today, he'll tell me to take you, to, to the security corps."

To the security corps, then so be it. I steeled my heart and replied, "I really don't have it today."

"No, no, you don't have it, is that right? Fine. You wait right here." As he spoke, he stood up. My wife, afraid that he would grab me, quickly stood up between us. But he waved his hand, stepped out of my door in two strides, and left. My wife looked at me with frightened eyes, as if asking, what will he do?

He left so quickly today, which made me really uneasy. I twisted the straw rope that was almost finished, my heart thumping. What will happen today?······

My wife didn't know when she had left the child in the straw nest. He suddenly started to cry loudly. I called out to her, but there was no response. I got angry, jumped up, and rushed to the back to find her. However, she wasn't there. I looked out the back door, the small river behind the house was flowing gurglingly. The wind blew the willow branches, rustling. A group of refugees ran over from the other side in a panic. San'er, from next door, holding a whip, breathing heavily, saw me and stopped at a distance, shouting:

"Hurry, hurry, your wife jumped into the river."

My heart panicked, and my legs went weak. But I dragged my heavy legs, following San'er. The shore of the harbor was already full of people. I saw someone pull her out of the water, her clothes soaking wet, clinging tightly to her body, her head hanging backward.

It's over, it's over! Before I could reach her, I felt something in my chest, hard and painful. I went up to take a look. Alas, her eyes were closed, her belly bloated with water, lying on the ground. I heard someone say: "She's dead. It was too long."

My chest seemed to explode, and I opened my mouth and vomited. I heard someone shout: Blood!

I fell to the ground.······

I vaguely saw Ah Da with his menacing face standing in the crowd. I shouted angrily: "This is a blood debt!"

Ordinary Matters (26) 296

— 𣽃 Guo Sun

"Has your household expenses increased or decreased lately compared to before?"

"Alas, how could they decrease!" Bo Tang sighed. "The other expenses haven't increased, but the cost of food has!"

"Roughly how much has it increased per month?"

"It's quite considerable! Now we have two meals a day, serving fourteen tables each time. Each table costs around 1.5 yuan, not counting the rice. That's 21 yuan a day, 630 yuan a month. The side dishes and rice cost over 200 yuan a month. So the food expenses alone are 900 yuan a month! Before, it was less than 200 yuan!"

"How long can you sustain this kind of provision?"

"Nobody knows! Presumably, the refugees here will stay until the fighting stops! Most of them have nowhere to move. The only option is to return home, but nobody is willing to go home anytime soon, and I would have to fund their journey back anyway. So, I need to prepare for at least two months' worth of food expenses."

"Adding in the other household expenses..."

"Then it's at least 1400 to 1500 yuan per month!"

"Preparing for two months would cost 3000 yuan!"

"That's just for my family. What about 'Old Master's' family?"

"How much is that per month?"

"Including household expenses and 'Old Master's' entertainment, it's at least 1500 yuan."

"At least?"

"Perhaps I should say approximately, because sometimes 'Old Master' is a bit more generous and tells me to delay payment for a while. But I should prepare that amount monthly to be ready when he asks."

"Then preparing for two months would also cost 3000 yuan!"

"Both items total 6000 yuan. Adding the 13000 yuan needed for the factory."

"That's the amount going out. How much can you expect to come in during these two months?"

"Coming in? Alas! As you know, the only income I can count on is the 3000 yuan that Zheng Yuan promised by the middle of this month. Aside from that, there's no other money coming in!"

"So, even with the 9000+ yuan from the factory, it's still less than 13000 yuan! If the situation continues, you'll need to find another solution!"

"There's no good solution here in this city! All our properties have been mortgaged. Although we could increase the mortgage, there's no way to manage the situation, so we can't increase the mortgage. Then we'll have to rely on pawnshops in other cities for help. However, the two branches in Nanjing and Zhenjiang have already put up 'stop pawning' signs because refugees are crowding the shops. They can't help us anymore. The branches in Ningbo and Shaoxing might have some surplus if the war zone doesn't expand further, but that's not something we can count on now."

As Bo Tang said this, Zhi Guang also felt dispirited. They looked at each other for a long time without saying a word. Finally, Bo Tang stood up, scratched his head, and then said as he sat down again:

Idle Talk

On the Higher-Class Chinese 297

— I

The term "Higher-Class Chinese" is certainly not as stiff and grating as "Long Gown Comrade," because the former is already documented in historical records. If scholars want to cite classics to support it, it's very easy.

However, Higher-Class Chinese and Long Gown Comrades are quite different, and it's obvious. Just in terms of appearance, Higher-Class Chinese often love to wear Western suits, looking no different from foreigners. And they are very particular about it. For example, they should wear light colors in the summer, brown or gray in the autumn, and naturally dark gray or black in the winter. As for matching ties and shoes with the color of the suit, that goes without saying. But that's not to say that every Higher-Class Chinese loves to wear Western suits. To show their Chinese identity, the long gown and Mandarin jacket are also suitable, but only occasionally.

Higher-Class Chinese can mostly say a few words of English, and are good at interacting with foreigners politely, bowing and shaking hands. Most of them have traveled abroad, or even studied there for a few years. They know quite a bit about foreign cultures and scenery. For example, they know that Christmas is on December 25th, and that day is Foreign Qingming, and they probably won't get it wrong. Their speech and actions can't lack some foreign air. Because they are used to interacting with foreign masters, they carry walking sticks, wear crooked hats, and the most fashionable thing is to replace "Alright" with "OK."

Needless to say, they are subordinate to the power of foreigners. To put it more plainly, they are happy to work in foreign companies and firms in China. Secondarily, they are also willing to invest in joint Sino-foreign ventures. As for their own silver ingots and paper money, they must be deposited in foreign banks, because they seem to be more reliable. They are always very indignant about the chaos and mess of everything Chinese. They certainly curse and swear at bandits and kidnapping, and they can't help but hiss at the rustic Chinese officials. They often have an unspeakable pain when it comes to the recovery of roads built beyond the concession or the recovery of the concession, because it is both inconvenient to openly oppose it, and they are very worried about the weakening of the power of imperialism. Fortunately, high-profile calls for the recovery of roads built beyond the concession, etc., are just sung occasionally and will never really become reality, so they don't have to end up depressed. In short, they are interdependent with blue-eyed and high-nosed foreigners. As long as the power of foreigners in China is not eliminated, their existence will be extended by one day.

Clearly, they have significant power in China. Scolding servants in English is just the most ordinary expression of their authority. Most importantly, powerful officials also rely on them.

Since the Chinese are called Higher-Class, then their lives are naturally superior. For example, the "First Class" section of the tram is set up for them and their masters. They live in three-story houses, drive Buick cars, and take their concubines for a spin on the Great Fool Garden Road, which is both leisurely and spirited. Alas, alas, that's all.

Literature and Art

Summer Evening Scene at Every Amusement Park 298

—

Electric lights shine brightly in the night sky, pleasing to the eye and endlessly delightful. To relax should also be done economically, coming here only costs twenty cents.

Crimson and green, lovely and beautifully made-up women, bustling back and forth, what are they so busy with? The fashionable modern women compete to dazzle, the gentle breeze stirs the fragrance of powder and rouge.

Thin silk dresses dragging on the ground, fragrant skin slightly exposed, a shimmering silhouette. Bees and butterflies vie to chase after them, stirring up sour winds and jealous waves.

Beijing opera, spoken drama and Shanghai opera, melodies are novel and also improved. A hundred kinds of shows are good to enjoy, but do you remember that Eastern China has already fallen?

My View on Long Gown Comrades 299

— Bing

After reading your piece, Mr. Wu, on "Long Gown Comrades," perhaps because I am somewhat close to this group, I couldn't help but feel disoriented for a long time, as Mr. Wu has scratched an itch.

Indeed, the current Long Gown Comrades, their lives, their consciousness, everything about them, are acting in extreme contradiction. But we need to ask a question: why are they so contradictory? The answer is: "This is created by the times!" Furthermore, what is the social foundation of all this? Where is the fundamental mistake? This seems to require some systematic explanation.

In the past, in China, so-called Long Gown Comrades, although not all belonged to the ranks of Hedda, wearing red hats and flower dragon robes, and going in and out of temples and palaces, certainly had a solid foundation in society. This foundation was the vast and wealthy countryside. They gathered the spit of ancient sages, infiltrated the "virtuous governance" of the heavenly king (?), and specialized in cultural suppression and anesthesia for the ruling class. In other words, the Long Gown Comrades of past dynasties were all spokespersons for the royal family, demons that suppressed freedom of thought, snails that maintained the status quo, using this to seek rewards from the emperor. Occasionally, they would be lucky and climb the dragon gate to occupy important positions. Otherwise, they could also raise the ghostly flags of "scholars" and "recommended men," running rampant in society, flaunting and deceiving, sucking the blood and sweat of farmers throughout their lives.

This is the "scholar-official class" that Professor Tao Xisheng never tires of talking about. According to historical laws, this kind of person can only exist and have power in a feudal society based on an agricultural economy. When it declines to a commercial capitalist society, it can still maintain its life to a considerable extent. But once it reaches today's industrial and commercial capitalist society, they are like beads cut off from a string, blindly rolling without direction. Capitalists do not

need you, the capacity of the ruling class is also limited, and they are most afraid of you not being loyal. In this situation, they cannot go up, nor can they go down. Retreating is not worth it, and advancing lacks courage. They hesitate and wander all day long. Therefore, their lives, their consciousness, everything about them, fall into contradiction.

Because they wear long gowns, they are ultimately smarter than people of the lower classes. Their desire for a comfortable life is almost comparable to that of the upper classes. They don't care about the millions of people living below them, only dreaming of their own comfort day and night. However, because the upper-class capitalists heavily exploit them, their desires cannot be fulfilled. Therefore, they suffer, suffer, and cry endlessly.

Because of this contradiction in life, their consciousness becomes even more radically contradictory. They uphold false and reserved pretenses, deliberately learning from the upper classes. Even if they cannot rise, they still want to maintain their own class. When this wish cannot be achieved, they experience another degree of pain in consciousness.

Indeed, the current Long Gown Comrades are extremely pained, and the future is even more pitch black.

But if they can thoroughly awaken and strive for self-rescue, it is not entirely without hope. However, they first need to recognize two points:

㈠ The old "scholar-official class" has already declined, and the possibility of establishing a new "scholar-official class" is absolutely impossible. Don't dream of class preservation, let alone dream of class

Page 22

ascension, because this is a dream that the times will absolutely not allow. They should urgently transform their petty bourgeois consciousness into proletarian consciousness, or at least pursue in that direction.

㈡ The evolution of society is gradually trending towards technical expertise. Relying solely on empty talk is no longer practical. They should use the spirit of labor to pursue a general technical life, not being ashamed to join hands with laborers, not being ashamed to be with laborers, and exposing all their hypocrisy.

In this way, even if the times are turbulent and shaking, I think the Long Gown Comrades will not only not be terrified in the future, but will also be able to stand firmly in a corner of society.

Excerpt from "Modern Master Calligraphy and Painting XXIII" 300

Mr. Tang Yifang's Painting (Brief Biography)

Tang Chao, styled Yifang, alias Li, learned his trade at a money shop in his youth. He studied landscape painting for five or six years. When Mr. Fei Junlong and Ding saw his work, they were amazed, considering him a reincarnation of Shi Gu (Wang Hui). They took him to Shancao Studio to meet Master Feng Chaoran. The master instructed him in everything. Mr. Tang possessed inherent wisdom and grasped things uniquely. He mastered landscape, flowers, birds, figures, and elegant women, receiving all the master's teachings. His reputation flourished, and those seeking his paintings flocked to his door daily. Since the Daoguang and Xianfeng eras, the Yun Jian School has been revived by Bu Yi Zhang Shangshu, who was able to invigorate the declining tradition and benefit scholars. Now, Tang is the most promising successor. Mr. Tang excels in iron-brush calligraphy, deeply capturing the essence of Ding and Deng.

Fiction

Under the Traffic Lights (39) 301

— Xi Lin

The car sped towards the countryside, which was already rippling with the verdant green of spring. The warm air of the sun had summoned many birds and bees into the air, and the languid wind blew willows wrapped around the white plums. Li Shiqiao kept looking out the car window. It was as if he was seeing spring, the beautiful spring, for the first time. He even doubted that this gentle spring still existed in the world. A new hope infused his blood, which had long been stiffened by the smoky city. He almost wanted to shout for joy.

In the fields, he gradually saw the seemingly endless, winding trenches with barbed wire lines. This was the second line of defense for our army. He secretly admired this great project, and the remnants under the traffic lights in the rear made him curse even more deeply. At this time, he was almost at the Jiangwan position. Looking back at the ruins of the defeated Zhabei, the cannon marks on the rooftops, and the piles of rubble, he couldn't help but clench his teeth and mutter to himself, "We will have our day of revenge!"

(22) Zhou Shaoping on the Front Lines

He got out of the car at the student volunteer army's encampment. The first thing he did was look for Zhou Shaoping, but Zhou Shaoping was no longer in the student army. Because most of the student soldiers lacked practical training and were mainly driven by temporary enthusiasm, they were not suitable for combat. Now the student volunteer army had been reassigned to relief work. Zhou Shaoping, however, was distressed by the fact that he could not go to the front lines, so he independently requested to join the third regiment of the XX division for formal combat. When Li Shiqiao found the position of the third regiment, the soldiers there were crouching in the newly dug yellow earth, fiercely chewing on their meager rations. Only a few sentries remained on the parapet, keeping watch.

On the left, a lookout post was also set up in a small thicket of "extracting trees" to monitor further afield. Suddenly, a squadron of three enemy planes was spotted in the upper right sky. The sound of the planes was unusually heavy, clearly a squadron of bombers. The sentries waved their hands, and the soldiers walking on the road all lay flat on the ground. Li Shiqiao naturally did the same. When the roaring of the planes gradually faded away, he stood up and walked towards the trenches again, but a soldier was walking towards him and saluting him from afar. He focused his attention and realized that the soldier was Zhou Shaoping. The enthusiasm of their meeting was needless to say, they held hands tightly for several minutes without letting go. Zhou Shaoping had completely transformed into an old soldier. The pale shadow of a scholar could no longer be seen in his dark skin. He couldn't help but sincerely praise Zhou Shaoping: "You are truly a young national hero!"

Zhou Shaoping also sincerely returned the compliment:

"Your work in the rear is also very successful! Didn't you invite many people to organize the XXXXX Federation of Organizations?"
